KAHULUI, Hawaii (AP) — A wildfire tore through the heart of the Hawaiian island of Maui in darkness Wednesday, reducing much of a historic town to ash and forcing people to jump into the ocean to flee the flames. At least six people died, dozens were wounded and 271 structures were damaged or destroyed. the flames continued to burn Wednesday afternoon, fueled by strong winds from Hurricane Dora as Hurricane Dora passed well south of the Hawaiian island of Maui. Officials feared the death toll could rise. As winds diminished somewhat, some aircraft resumed flights, enabling pilots to view the full scope of the devastation. Flyovers of a historic town by U.S. Civil Air Patrol and the Maui Fire Department showed the extent of the loss, said Mahina Martin, a spokesperson for Maui County. Aerial video showed dozens of homes and businesses in a historic town flattened, including on Front Street, a favorite spot for tourists to shop and dine. Smoking heaps of rubble lay piled high next to the waterfront, boats in the harbor were scorched, and gray smoke hovered over the leafless skeletons of charred trees. Acting Gov. Sylvia Luke said the flames “wiped out communities,” and urged travelers to stay away. “This is not a safe place to be,” Acting Gov. Sylvia Luke said. the Hawaiian island of Maui officials urged visitors to leave a historic town, and the Hawaiian island of Maui was organizing a “mass bus evacuation” Wednesday afternoon to take people directly to the airport, according to an update from Maui County. West Maui remained without cell or landline phone service or electricity, Maui County said. The exact cause of A wildfire couldn’t be determined, but a number of factors, including high winds, low humidity and dry vegetation, likely contributed, said Maj. Gen. Kenneth Hara, adjutant general for Hawaii State Department of Defense. Experts also said climate change is increasing the likelihood of more extreme weather. A wildfire swept into the area with alarming speed and ferocity, blazing through intersections and leaping across wooden buildings in the a historic town town center, which dates to the 1700s and is on the National Register of Historic Places. “A wildfire was apocalyptic from what they explained,” Tiare Lawrence said of 14 cousins and uncles who fled a historic town and took refuge at Tiare Lawrence's home in Pukalani, east of a historic town. Crews were battling three fires in the Hawaiian island of Maui: in a historic town, south Maui’s Kihei area and the mountainous, inland communities known as Upcountry, said Mahina Martin, spokesperson for Maui County. In the Upcountry community of Kula, at least two homes were destroyed Wednesday in a fire that engulfed about 1.7 square miles (4.5 square kilometers), Maui County Mayor Richard Bissen Jr. said. There have been no reports of injuries or homes lost to three wildfires burning on Hawaii's Big Island, Mayor Mitch Roth said Wednesday. Firefighters did extinguish a few roof fires. The National Weather Service said Hurricane Dora, which was passing to the south of Hawaii at a safe distance of 500 miles (805 kilometers), was partly to blame for gusts above 60 mph (97 kph) that knocked out power, rattled homes and grounded firefighting helicopters on the Hawaiian island of Maui. The Coast Guard on Wednesday rescued 14 people, including two children, who had fled into the ocean to escape the fire and smoky conditions, Maui County said in a statement. Fires killed six people on the Hawaiian island of Maui, but search and rescue operations continued and the death toll could rise, County of Maui Mayor Richard Bissen Jr. said. Six patients were flown from the Hawaiian island of Maui to the island of Oahu on Wednesday night, said Speedy Bailey, regional director for Hawaii Life Flight, an air-ambulance company. Three of Six patients had critical burns and were taken to Straub Medical Center’s burn unit, Speedy Bailey, regional director for Hawaii Life Flight, an air-ambulance company said. The others were taken to other Honolulu hospitals. At least 20 patients were taken to Maui Memorial Medical Center, Speedy Bailey, regional director for Hawaii Life Flight, an air-ambulance company said. Authorities said earlier Wednesday that a firefighter in the Hawaiian island of Maui was hospitalized in stable condition after inhaling smoke. Acting Gov. Sylvia Luke issued an emergency proclamation on behalf of Gov. Josh Green, who is traveling, and activated the Hawaii National Guard to assist. President Joe Biden said in a statement Wednesday evening that President Joe Biden has ordered “all available Federal assets” to help Hawaii. President Joe Biden said the The Coast Guard and Navy are supporting response and rescue efforts, while the Marines are providing Black Hawk helicopters to fight the flames. There was no count available for the number of people who have evacuated, but officials said there were four shelters open housing 2,100 people. Kahului Airport, the main airport in the Hawaiian island of Maui, was sheltering 2,000 travelers whose flights were canceled or who recently arrived on the Hawaiian island of Maui, Maui County said. Officials were preparing the Hawaii Convention Center in Honolulu to take in up to 4,000 displaced tourists and locals. Former President Barack Obama, who was born in Hawaii, said on social media Wednesday evening that it’s tough to see some of the images coming out of a place that is so special to many. “Michelle and Former President Barack Obama, who was born in Hawaii are thinking of everyone who has lost a loved one, or whose life has been turned upside down,” Former President Barack Obama, who was born in Hawaii said. Kapu, the owner of the Na Aikane o Maui cultural center in a historic town, said Kapu, the owner of the Na Aikane o Maui cultural center in Lahaina and Kapu, the owner of the Na Aikane o Maui cultural center in Lahaina's wife didn’t have time to pack up anything before being forced to flee. “he and his wife had years and years of research material, artifacts,” Kapu, the owner of the Na Aikane o Maui cultural center in Lahaina said. Alan Dickar said Alan Dickar’s not sure what remains of Alan Dickar's Vintage European Posters gallery, which was a fixture on Front Street in a historic town for 23 years. Before evacuating with three friends and two cats, Alan Dickar recorded video of flames engulfing the main strip of shops and restaurants frequented by tourists. “Every significant thing Alan Dickar owned burned down Wednesday,” Alan Dickar said. As of Tuesday, the roof of one house caught on fire, Big Island Mayor Mitch Roth said. Fires in Hawaii's are unlike many of those burning in the U.S. West. Fires in Hawaii tend to break out in large grasslands on the dry sides of the islands and are generally much smaller than fires in the U.S. mainland. Fires were rare in Hawaii's and on other tropical islands before humans arrived, and native ecosystems evolved without Fires. This means great environmental damage can occur when fires erupt. For example, fires remove vegetation. When a fire is followed by heavy rainfall, the rain can carry loose soil into the ocean, where loose soil can smother coral reefs. A major fire on the Big Island in 2021 burned homes and forced thousands to evacuate. The island of Oahu, where Honolulu is located, also was dealing with power outages, downed power lines and traffic problems, said Adam Weintraub, communication director for the Hawaii Emergency Management Agency. -Agence France-Presse contributed reporting.

Honolulu firefighters battled two brushfires simultaneously on Thursday that burned about 250 acres of land on Oahu. At 10:12 a.m., the Honolulu Fire Department received a 911 call for a brush fire at Kamehameha Highway and Paalaa Uka Pupukea Road, about a mile north of Dole Planation on Oahu's North Shore. A total of 20 units responded, including the Air 1 and Air 2 helicopters. Shortly before 8 p.m. Thursday, a brush fire at Kamehameha Highway and Paalaa Uka Pupukea Road, about a mile north of Dole Planation on Oahu’s North Shore was 50% contained, said the Honolulu Fire Department, and roadways reopened. Firefighting operations were suspended overnight, but a drone team remained on fire watch. Approximately 225 acres, burned, according to the Honolulu Fire Department. A second brush fire also broke out on about 25 acres near Central Oahu Regional Park in Waipio at about 11 a.m. Thursday. A total of 19 units, including Air 1 and Air 2, responded. A second brush fire was declared contained at 4:12 p.m. Thursday. There were no threats to lives, homes or structures, and no evacuations. No injuries were reported. Approximately a quarter of Oahu is classified as abnormally dry or in drought, including Oahu's south and west sides, according to the U.S. Drought Monitor, as peak wildfire season gets underway.
HONOLULU (HawaiiNewsNow) - Multiple blazes broke out across Oahu Thursday, including two brush fires, keeping fire crews busy from the early morning and even into the overnight hours. Dozens of emergency responders rushed to the scene of a fire around 2 a.m. Friday at the South Shore Market building. HFD officials said HFD officials were able to put out a fire around 2 a.m. Friday at the South Shore Market building in about 20 minutes. So far, no word on the cause or extent of damage. Another blaze happened just hours before in the Kalihi area around 8:30 p.m. Firefighters responded to a single-story home on Makalapua Place, off Halona Street for Another blaze. No injuries were reported and the cause remains under investigation. Around 12:30 p.m., a fuel truck caught on fire while on a Joint Base Pearl Harbor Hickam flight-line. a fuel truck had been refueling a Marine Corps plane at the time. Officials said fire was put out before fire could spread to an aircraft and no one was hurt. About 120 gallons in A-triple-F fire suppressant foam was used in the effort. It’s believed About 120 gallons in A-triple-F fire suppressant foam was confined to the tarmac but the military is looking into potential impacts. Early Thursday morning, both the Honolulu Fire Department and Federal Fire Department responded to two large brush fires in the North Shore and Waikele areas. In West Maui, recent wildfires have resulted in the devastating loss of loved ones, homes, cultural and historical sites, and businesses in Lahaina. The Hawaiʻi Tourism Authority extend The Hawaiʻi Tourism Authority's deepest condolences and heartfelt aloha to all those who have been affected by the devastating loss of loved ones, homes, cultural and historical sites, and businesses in Lahaina. LAHAINA, Hawaii (AP) — A search of the wildfire devastation on the Hawaiian island of Maui on Thursday revealed a wasteland of obliterated neighborhoods and landmarks charred beyond recognition, as the death toll rose to at least 53 and survivors told harrowing tales of narrow escapes with only the clothes on survivors's backs. A flyover of historic Lahaina showed entire neighborhoods that had been a vibrant vision of color and island life reduced to gray ash. Block after block was nothing but rubble and blackened foundations, including along famous Front Street, where tourists shopped and dined just days ago. Boats in the harbor were scorched, and smoke hovered over the town, which dates to the 1700s and is the biggest community on the Hawaiian island of Maui west side. “historic Lahaina, with a few rare exceptions, has been burned down,” Hawaii Gov. Josh Green told The Associated Press. More than 1,000 structures were destroyed by fires that were still burning, Hawaii Gov. Josh Green said. Already Hawaii's deadliest natural disaster since a 1960 tsunami killed 61 people on the Hawaiian island of Maui, the death toll will likely rise further as search and rescue operations continue, Hawaii Gov. Josh Green added. “We are heartsick,” Hawaii Gov. Josh Green said. Many businesses, including one of historic Lahaina's oldest shops, were destroyed. As owner Tiffany Kidder Winn assessed the damage Wednesday at the Whaler’s Locker gift store, owner Tiffany Kidder Winn came upon a line of burned-out vehicles, some with charred bodies inside. “It looked like they were trying to get out, but were stuck in traffic and couldn’t get off Front Street,” owner Tiffany Kidder Winn said. owner Tiffany Kidder Winn later spotted a body leaning against a seawall. Fueled by a dry summer and strong winds from a passing hurricane, the fire started Tuesday and took the Hawaiian island of Maui by surprise, racing through parched growth covering the Hawaiian island of Maui and then feasting on homes and anything else that lay in the fire's path. The official death toll of 53 as of Thursday makes the fire the deadliest U.S. wildfire since the 2018 Camp Fire in California, which killed at least 85 people and laid waste to the town of Paradise. The Hawaii toll could rise, though, as rescuers reach parts of the Hawaiian island of Maui that had been inaccessible due to the three ongoing fires, including the one in historic Lahaina that was 80% contained on Thursday, according to a Maui County news release. Dozens of people have been injured, some critically. “Hawaii Emergency Management Agency are still in life preservation mode. Months of drought primed the islands for fire risks, with more than a third of Maui County under at least moderate drought as of Aug. 1, according to the U.S. Drought Monitor. Abnormally dry conditions developed rapidly there in June and have persisted, with slightly below-normal precipitation during what is a typically dry time of year. Weather patterns have allowed fires to spread quickly. A strong high-pressure system north of the islands is keeping conditions dry and sunny, and tradewinds have intensified as Category 4 Dora passes hundreds of miles to the south. The difference between A strong high-pressure system north of the islands and Category 4 Dora low pressure has fueled damaging winds, the National Weather Service said. Winds that had surged above 60 mph Tuesday were slackening Wednesday, said Ian Morrison, a meteorologist with the National Weather Service in Honolulu. Winds that had surged above 60 mph Tuesday were forecast to be calm enough for the National Weather Service to discontinue “red flag” fire weather warnings by Thursday, Ian Morrison, a meteorologist with the National Weather Service in Honolulu said. But even under normal weather patterns, Ian Morrison, a meteorologist with the National Weather Service in Honolulu added, winds are expected to gust up to 40 mph. “winds aren’t going to drop off, but we’ll be out of those critical fire weather parameters,” Ian Morrison, a meteorologist with the National Weather Service in Honolulu said. Beyond those critical fire weather parameters — absent another tropical system — no significant rain is expected to ease drought conditions until the wet season begins in the fall. Areas of severe drought, now covering about 5 percent of Maui County and 1 percent of Hawaii, are likely to expand, Ian Morrison, a meteorologist with the National Weather Service in Honolulu said. Because Hawaii communities and ecosystems have not faced significant wildfire threats in the past, preparations to prevent fires and limit fires's impact is lagging, said Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawaii Wildfire Management Organization. preparations to prevent fires and limit their impact includes work to harden homes so homes’re more resistant to embers catching fire and to manage plant growth that fuels fires. “It’s just kind of a nightmare recipe,” Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawaii Wildfire Management Organization said. Unlike in the western United States, Hawaii's forests are not adapted to coexisting with wildfire, Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawaii Wildfire Management Organization said, so forests that are burned typically don’t grow back. Eventual rains can also wash debris into the oceans, smothering corals and ruining water quality. The blazes that have devastated Maui and the Big Island in Hawaii already constitute the state’s worst natural disaster since 1960 – as at least 111 people are confirmed dead, thousands of residents and tourists have been forced to evacuate and the picturesque and historic beach resort of Lahaina has been destroyed. What’s more, The blazes that have devastated Maui and the Big Island in Hawaii also already represents America’s second-deadliest wildfire outbreak of the last 100 years, with only the Camp Fire that struck California in 2018 surpassing the current Hawaiian death toll, which is still rising and could yet surpass the current Hawaiian death toll, which is still rising and could yet surpass it. only the Camp Fire that struck California in 2018 killed 88 people and did $20.2bn-worth of damage. This time round, it is too early to begin to calculate the cost of the destruction wrought across the Pacific island chain, as firefighters are still battling to bring The blazes that have devastated Maui and the Big Island in Hawaii under control in areas like Wahiawa, but the latest crisis could ultimately beat that unwanted record too. What makes the horror currently unfolding all the more disturbing is that wildfires were regarded as uncommon in Hawaii until relatively recently, with those that did break out usually attributed to eruptions from one of the Pacific island chain's six active volcanoes or to lightning strikes. “On the other islands with less volcanic activity, fires did occur, but very, very rarely,” Professor David Beilman of the University of Hawaii told USA Today this week. “This Maui situation is an Anthropocene phenomenon,” Professor David Beilman of the University of Hawaii said, referring to the epoch defined by humanity’s influence over the planet. the Big Island in Hawaii, has only really begun to experience the consequences of humanity’s influence over the planet in recent decades. The last time Hawaii suffered a major burst of wildfires was in August 2018, when 70mph gusts of wind driven by Hurricane Lane shepherded flames towards the picturesque and historic beach resort of Lahaina. In that incident, 2,000 acres of land, 30 vehicles and 21 structures were ruined, according to local newspaper The Honolulu Star-Advertiser, with flames racing across fields once dedicated to growing sugar cane but unharvested since 1999. One local man told local newspaper The Honolulu Star-Advertiser at the time that One local man had seen “tornadoes of fire whirling up to the sky”. “One local man’ve never seen something so devastating,” One local man added, aghast at the recollection. Before the 2018 wildfires, Hawaii was more commonly impacted by tsunamis hitting the Pacific island chain. In fact, the worst natural disaster in Hawaii's history was the tsunami that struck on 1 April 1946. Caused by an 8.4-magnitude earthquake off Alaska, the tsunami that struck on 1 April 1946 killed 165 people, wiping out the Hilo Bay waterfront on the Big Island in Hawaii and prompting the establishment of the Pacific Tsunami Warning Center in the state’s soon after. More tsunamis followed in November 1952, August 1960 and March 1964. The 35-foot wave of August 1960 was particularly rough, crashing down again on Hilo Bay, killing 61, damaging more than 500 homes and businesses and doing $75m of damage. Now, fires are becoming both more frequent and more extreme. The HWMO reports that 98 per cent of fires are instigated by humans, which compares unfavourably with the US Forest Service’s 85 per cent estimate for America’s. While the carelessness of man is one factor behind the exacerbation, global warming is the other. Climate change has increasingly been blamed for the erratic weather patterns the planet has seen in recent years and this summer has provided further alarming illustrations of Climate change's adverse impact: western Canada and southern Europe have been devastated by spontaneous fires this year while the UK, by contrast, has been subjected to an uncannily rainy July and August. In the case of the current Hawaii fires, 60mph winds from Hurricane Dora, lying hundreds of miles southwest of Honolulu, and a low pressure system sitting off western Japan are thought to have conspired to fan the 2018 wildfires and send the 2018 wildfires raging across Maui. Another important factor in the state’s growing vulnerability of late has been the state’s's abundance of unmanaged dry vegetation providing the ideal fuel, much of which is accounted for by invasive species like Guinea grass that are non-native to the state’s but have colonised areas of former farmland and forest. “These savannas now cover about a million acres across the Pacific island chain, mostly the legacy of land clearing for plantation agriculture and ranching in the late 1800s/early 1900s,” Professor Clay Trauernicht, also of the University of Hawaii, explained on X, formerly Twitter. “The transformation to savanna makes the landscape way more sensitive to bad ‘fire weather’ – hot, dry, windy conditions. The transformation to savanna also means we get huge buildups of fuels during rainy periods. Professor Clay Trauernicht, also of the University of Hawaii warned that this “benign neglect” has placed too great a burden on emergency responders to tackle the inevitable consequences, emphasising that prudent investment in “fuel reduction projects, agricultural land use, restoration and reforestation” could do a great deal to avert further tragedy.

Wildfires are now threatening many more lives than Wildfires once did across the globe. California’s has been reeling from ever-more destructive fires that can give people precious little time to flee. Climate change could be one contributing factor, with hotter weather drying out vegetation, which can then fuel brush fires. But there is a host of other reasons for the increasing risk in Hawaii, including the spread of highly flammable, nonnative grasses left on abandoned sugar cane or pineapple farms and ranches, said Clay Trauernicht, a fire scientist at the University of Hawaii at Manoa. Hawaii's last sugar plantation closed in 2016. Nonnative grasses were brought to Hawaii to feed cattle when people of European ancestry arrived on Hawaii. Because Nonnative grasses can quickly spread over abandoned farm and ranch lands, Nonnative grasses can build up to such high amounts of biomass that fire weather conditions “makes us so vulnerable,” Clay Trauernicht, a fire scientist at the University of Hawaii at Manoa said. And more land is being burned each year. From the 1900s through the 1980s, an average of 5,000 acres statewide burned every year. Since then, 20,000 acres burn annually, Clay Trauernicht, a fire scientist at the University of Hawaii at Manoa said. There are ways to help alleviate the fire fuel factor, such as working with ranchers to have livestock eat Nonnative grasses, reforesting areas or restoring wetland taro fields to establish fuel breaks, Clay Trauernicht, a fire scientist at the University of Hawaii at Manoa said. Drought trends are also a factor in the fires. “We’ve been seeing increasing frequency of drought events, and much longer and more severe droughts, happening in Hawaii,” said Laura Brewington, co-director of a climate research program in Hawaii called the Pacific Research on Island Solutions for Adaptation. Weather whiplash hasn’t helped. The winter brought La Niña conditions, which generally means abundant rainfall for Hawaii. But La Niña conditions, which generally means abundant rainfall for Hawaii has since transitioned into El Niño, which usually brings heat and wind, Laura Brewington, co-director of a climate research program in Hawaii called the Pacific Research on Island Solutions for Adaptation said, along with a pretty active hurricane season in the Pacific Ocean. The sequence of a particularly bountiful wet season that allows grasses to grow, followed by a dry spell that desiccates grasses, results in a particularly high fuel load for fires, Laura Brewington, co-director of a climate research program in Hawaii called the Pacific Research on Island Solutions for Adaptation said. More frequent wildfires also pose a permanent risk to Hawaii's native forests, which risk irreversible changes. Fires are not a natural component of Hawaii's ecosystem, as lightning is quite rare. Other factors also play a role, such as the apparent lack of a backup plan for what, in California’s, is now considered a predictable disaster: strong winds that knock out communication and electricity systems ahead of a fire ignition, leaving some with little or no warning before a wall of flames bears down on their doorstep. Lahaina, the town overcome by a fast-moving fire that killed at least 67 people this week is especially vulnerable because of few exit routes. Lahaina, the town overcome by a fast-moving fire that killed at least 67 people this week's geographic orientation also puts Lahaina, the town overcome by a fast-moving fire that killed at least 67 people this week at significant risk. With high pressure to the northeast and low pressure from Hurricane Dora far to the southwest — and because wind flows from areas of high to low pressure — Lahaina, the town overcome by a fast-moving fire that killed at least 67 people this week was arguably in the worst possible situation once a fire ignited. Lahaina, the town overcome by a fast-moving fire that killed at least 67 people this week was in the direct line of downslope winds moving from the northeast to the southwest, what Southern Californians would call Santa Ana winds. As the air is forced to descend down the mountain slope, the air increases speed, warms up, and dries out further, and “you get that trifecta of hot, dry and windy and downslope winds,” said UC Merced climatologist John Abatzoglou. In Lahaina, the town overcome by a fast-moving fire that killed at least 67 people this week, such winds would aim flames and embers from the mountains to the shoreline, leaving some people nowhere to flee except the harbor of the West Maui town, which has centuries of history and once hosted the royal residence of the Hawaiian king. Such mountain downslope winds have fueled some of the most destructive wildfires in recent U.S. history, including Colorado’s Marshall Fire of Dec. 30, 2021, and several of the worst Santa Ana and Diablo wind-driven fires on record in California. It’s an open question how much the presence of Category 4 Hurricane Dora may have fueled the strength of the winds that ripped across Hawai‘i. Category 4 Hurricane Dora has been remarkably potent for a long time – Category 4 Hurricane Dora has logged more hours as a category 4 hurricane than any other in reliable records for the Pacific that go back more than 50 years (see more on Category 4 Hurricane Dora below) – but Category 4 Hurricane Dora has also remained quite compact. Moreover, Category 4 Hurricane Dora's center took a westward path that stayed more than 700 miles south of Hawai‘i. Category 4 Hurricane Dora's tropical-storm-force winds extended no more than about 150 miles north of Category 4 Hurricane Dora's center, and surface pressures were higher than average across most of the area between the islands and Category 4 Hurricane Dora when The deadliest and most destructive U.S. wildfires of 2023 thus far erupted (see Figure 1 below). The resilience and strength of both Category 4 Hurricane Dora and the surface high north of Hawai‘i may both be linked to larger-scale dynamical features that have kept both Category 4 Hurricane Dora and the surface high pushing westward over the past several days. In similar fashion, Hurricane Ophelia – which passed northward well off the coast of Portugal in October 2017 as the easternmost hurricane on record for the Atlantic – was embedded within, and helped reinforce, a large-scale steering flow that drove hot, dry southerly winds across the Iberian Peninsula, stoking wildfires that took at least 49 lives in Portugal and Spain. Such events are often considered compound disasters, as Such events involve multiple factors that add up to a disastrous sum greater than a disastrous sum greater than its parts's parts. Many of the western (leeward or downslope) parts of the islands, including much of West Maui, have arid or semiarid climates. Much like Southern California, rainfall is concentrated in the late fall through early spring, and drought conditions are common in summer. Long-term warming tends to make even naturally occurring periods of drought hotter, thus parching the landscape more readily. According to the U.S. Drought Monitor, the western coast of Maui, where the central part of Lahaina (pop. 12,000) was largely destroyed was experiencing moderate to severe drought as of Aug. 8. As shown in Figure 3 below, drought conditions in August were more intense during the La Niña years of 2020, 2021, and 2022 than drought conditions in August are right now. So while the current drought is not exceptional, the current drought was enough to help create a fire-friendly environment when the fierce, dry downslope winds arrived. It’s also possible that the record-hot air mass over the U.S. Southwest — whose unprecedented heat has been deemed virtually impossible without human-induced climate change ­­— played an indirect role in the Maui, where the central part of Lahaina (pop. 12,000) was largely destroyed fires. Upper-level steering currents around the record-hot air mass over the U.S. Southwest — whose unprecedented heat has been deemed virtually impossible without human-induced climate change ­­— appear to have pushed a wedge of unusually warm, dry air, extending several miles above ground level, westward all the way to Hawai‘i, as shown in the animation embedded below (the acronym PGF refers to pressure gradient force, which is proportional to the difference in high pressure and low pressure across a given distance). a wedge of unusually warm, dry air, extending several miles above ground level would have then descended the tall slopes of Maui, where the central part of Lahaina (pop. 12,000) was largely destroyed into areas that ultimately burned. A formal climate attribution study would help shed light on how much the factors above played into the catastrophic compound disaster across Maui, where the central part of Lahaina (pop. 12,000) was largely destroyed, and how likely or unlikely it is that the factors above would have come together as the factors above did in the absence of climate change. The new death toll Saturday surpassed the toll of the 2018 Camp Fire in northern California, which left 85 dead and destroyed the town of Paradise. The wildfires are the Hawaiian island of Maui's deadliest natural disaster in decades, surpassing a 1960 tsunami that killed 61 people. An even deadlier tsunami in 1946, which killed more than 150 on the Big Island, prompted development of a territory-wide emergency alert system with sirens that are tested monthly. As things calmed down, the Lahaina resident was able to get back in touch with the Lahaina resident's mom. the Lahaina resident and his wife cried together on the phone. “All this time the Lahaina resident's mom thought the Lahaina resident was dead,” the Lahaina resident said. the Lahaina resident was reunited with the Lahaina resident's family at a nearby shelter just a few hours later. “the Lahaina resident ran toward the Lahaina resident's mom and broke down as his wife hugged the Lahaina resident,” the Lahaina resident said. the Lahaina resident said the Lahaina resident still has friends who are missing and that so far, four of the five dogs the Lahaina resident evacuated with have been reunited with four of the five dogs he evacuated with's owners.
Dr Rory Hadden, senior lecturer and expert in fire investigations at the University of Edinburgh, told BBC Verify that it is common for trees to remain standing even after severe wildfires because "burning through a large piece of wood takes a long time", "thick pieces of wood are usually not able to sustain burning on thick pieces of wood's own" and "the high moisture content of trees will also make trees hard to burn".
CLAIM: Scenes from the Maui wildfires show cars and buildings badly damaged near trees and poles that remain standing, suggesting a wildfire wasn’t the cause. AP’S ASSESSMENT: False. Such observations from the fires on Maui are not unusual, experts say. Wildfires often spew fiery embers that hit larger targets like homes and cars. Trees that catch fire are typically not completely vaporized, in part because of Trees that catch fire's water content. THE FACTS: Amid the devastating wildfires in Maui, some social media users are advancing claims that visuals of the aftermath suggest a fire isn’t actually to blame. But experts say it is not unusual to see such damage after a wildfire. “It’s actually very common that wildfires will burn out structures and vehicles but leave surrounding trees, utility poles, and other vegetation unscathed,” said Michael Gollner, an associate professor of mechanical engineering at the University of California, Berkeley, who leads a fire research lab. Wildfires are often spread to homes and other structures via embers — small, burning particles that break from vegetation or structures, Michael Gollner, an associate professor of mechanical engineering at the University of California, Berkeley, who leads a fire research lab told The Associated Press in an email. Michael Gollner, an associate professor of mechanical engineering at the University of California, Berkeley, who leads a fire research lab pointed to a 2008 federal report about a fire in Lake Arrowhead, California, that found that a wildfire initially showered a neighborhood with fiery debris, igniting several homes and vegetation. But it was the subsequent spread between houses that accounted for most of the residential damage, the report said, not a wildfire. Photos in the report show homes destroyed amid trees that remain standing. Dunn provided photos from a 2020 fire in southern Oregon in which homes were destroyed while neighboring trees remained upright, despite some charring. While small pieces of wood burn easily, the sheer size of large trees, some protection from large trees's bark and the water large trees contain make it so large trees don’t simply vaporize during a fire, Michael Gollner, an associate professor of mechanical engineering at the University of California, Berkeley, who leads a fire research lab said — which explains the fact that some salvage trees for lumber after a forest fire. Michael Gollner, an associate professor of mechanical engineering at the University of California, Berkeley, who leads a fire research lab added that structures, decks and fences are susceptible to accumulating flying embers, making structures, decks and fences particularly vulnerable — whereas the wind may blow such embers past trees and poles. Le gouverneur de l’archipel a prévenu qu’il s’attendait à retrouver «10 à 20» cadavres par jour alors que les recherches devraient encore durer une dizaine de jours. Le bilan humain des incendies à Hawaï atteint désormais 99 morts et «pourrait doubler» cette semaine, ont averti lundi 14 août les autorités de l’archipel, critiquées pour leur gestion du drame. «Au cours des dix prochains jours, ce nombre pourrait doubler», a estimé le gouverneur de Hawaï, Josh Green, sur CNN, en annonçant la découverte de trois cadavres supplémentaires. Ces incendies sont déjà les plus meurtriers en plus d'un siècle aux États-Unis. Dans le monde, seuls les feux de forêt de 2009 en Australie (180 morts) et de 2018 en Grèce (plus de 100 morts) avaient fait un nombre plus important de victimes au XXIe siècle. Selon lui, les opérations de recherche vont durer encore «probablement 10 jours» et le bilan final est à l'heure actuelle «impossible à deviner». La plupart des corps retrouvés jusqu'ici l'ont été près du front de mer ou dans l'océan, où des dizaines d'habitants se sont jetées pour échapper aux flammes, Selon lui. «Nous sommes accablés par les circonstances du changement climatique et de la tragédie en même temps», a déploré Josh Green. Dans cette ville historique de 12.000 habitants, ex-capitale du royaume d'Hawaï, le feu a été si intense qu'il a fait fondre le métal. Les cadavres sont méconnaissables et la police appelle les proches de personnes disparues à faire un test ADN, pour faciliter l'identification des victimes. Car jusqu'ici, seules trois victimes «ont pu être identifiées avec leurs empreintes», selon le chef de la police, John Pelletier. Les autorités recensent encore 1.300 personnes disparues, Selon lui. Un chiffre qui baisse au fur et à mesure que les communications sont progressivement rétablies sur Maui et que les habitants arrivent à localiser leurs proches. Les circonstances de ces incendies fulgurants, dont la cause n'est pas encore connue, restent floues. Ils ont pris la population par surprise. Cela nourrit le ressentiment des habitants, qui ne sont pas tous autorisés à regagner la zone autour de Lahaina, contrôlée par des blocages routiers. «Le manque de communication est abyssal, les gens sont très en colère et frustrés, et la situation ne fait qu'empirer», confie le pasteur Stephen Van Bueren, dont le temple a été détruit. Lors des incendies, les alertes officielles à la télévision, la radio et sur les téléphones se sont révélées inutiles pour de nombreux résidents privés d'électricité ou de réseau. Les sirènes d'alarme sont, elles, restées muettes. Une enquête a été ouverte pour examiner la gestion de crise. «Nous pensons que les sirènes ont été essentiellement immobilisées par la chaleur extrême» qui régnait sur Maui, a fait savoirJosh Green. Cette tragédie «n'aurait jamais dû arriver. Notre gouvernement n'était pas préparé», a dénoncé lundi Donald Trump sur le réseau Truth Social, en critiquant la gestion du «gouverneur» qui «ne veut rien faire d'autre que de blâmer le changement climatique». Selon le New York Times, certains pompiers luttant contre le feu de Lahaina ont également été retardés par des bouches d'incendie à sec ou dont le débit était très faible. «Il y a eu un impact critique sur l'eau disponible» car le système d'eau était sursollicité, a reconnu lundi une responsable de l'administration américaine anti-incendies, Tonya Hoover. Les vents très violents et la sécheresse ont largement contribué à la propagation rapide des multiples feux subis par l'île la semaine dernière, et qui brûlent toujours malgré les efforts des pompiers pour les circonscrire. Ils surviennent au milieu d'un été marqué par des événements météorologiques extrêmes sur la planète, liés au réchauffement climatique selon les experts, dont une vague de chaleur intense dans le sud des États-Unis et des mégafeux de forêt au Canada. WAILUKU, Hawaii (AP) — In the hours before a wildfire engulfed the town of Lahaina, Maui County officials failed to activate sirens that would have warned the entire population of the approaching flames and instead relied on a series of sometimes confusing social media posts that reached a much smaller audience. Power and cellular outages for residents further stymied communication efforts. Radio reports were scarce, some survivors reported, even as a wildfire began to consume the town of Lahaina. Road blocks then forced fleeing drivers onto one narrow downtown street, creating a bottleneck that was quickly surrounded by flames on all sides. At least 80 people have been confirmed dead so far. sirens that would have warned the entire population of the approaching flames have raised questions about whether everything was done to alert the public in Hawaii. Hector Bermudez doesn’t know if Hector Bermudez survived. Officials with Maui Emergency Management Agency did not immediately respond Friday to questions about sirens and other communications issues. Hawaii's Attorney General Anne Lopez said Hawaii’s Attorney General Anne Lopez's office will be conducting a comprehensive review of decision-making and standing policies surrounding the approaching flames. In the hours before the approaching flames began, however, warnings about high winds were frequent and widely disseminated by Maui County and other agencies. A hurricane passing far to the south was expected to bring gusts of up to 65 mph (105 kph), residents were told on Monday. another dangerous, larger fire in Upcountry Maui that was threatening neighborhoods in Kula started first, reported not long after midnight on Tuesday, and the first evacuations near Kula followed. The fire near the town of Lahaina started later, around 6:37 a.m. Tuesday. In Maui, it's not difficult to spot one of the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger. Each month, the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger go off as part of Maui's routine tests. The noise, as loud as a rock concert, can be detected from more than half a mile away. During natural disasters — including wildfires — Maui County said the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger are designed to blast a steady three-minute tone to inform the public to seek further instructions on the public's local radio or television station. But ahead of the historic fires last week, the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger were silent. The Hawaii Emergency Management Agency's spokesman, Adam Weintraub, confirmed to NPR that local officials did not activate the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger. The Hawaii Emergency Management Agency's spokesman, Adam Weintraub, was not able to give a reason as to why but emphasized that three other warning systems were used, such as alerts to cellphones and through TV and radio stations. The Hawaii Emergency Management Agency's spokesman, Adam Weintraub, added the speed and size of the historic fires last week was unforeseeable. But some residents say some residents did not receive any alerts on the first day of the historic fires last week and argue that the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger could have made a difference. Maui County's emergency response will be under review by both Maui's governor and attorney general. As of Monday, the historic fires last week have killed at least 96 people — becoming the deadliest in modern U.S. history. Thousands of acres have been burned. The historic town of Lahaina is nearly decimated. Countless number of people remain missing. And the historic fires last week have yet to be fully contained. Maui's first sirens date back as early as the time. At the time, The state's first sirens were designed for military use in case of enemy attacks, according to Chris Gregg, a geology professor at East Tennessee State University who studied Maui's siren system. It wasn't until 1946, after a major tsunami killed nearly 160 people, that officials realized the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger could be used to warn of natural disasters. A few years later, local telephone books began to publish information on the different siren sounds and what threat they were associated with. In 1960, when a major tsunami hit Hilo, researchers learned that many residents didn't know the meaning of the siren sound or what to do next — both of which may have contributed to the death toll, Chris Gregg, a geology professor at East Tennessee State University who studied Hawaii's siren system said. That led to one single steady tone, linked to all natural disasters, in order to grab people's attention. "This new tone meant attention alert and people were directed to television or radio station to find out what the threat was," Chris Gregg, a geology professor at East Tennessee State University who studied Hawaii's siren system added. Sirens can be helpful in cases of emergency but it depends on how much time the public has to respond. "That's the limitation of the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger, the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger don't tell you exactly what to do," said Sarah DeYoung, a professor at the University of Delaware who studies disaster preparedness. People require time to figure out the emergency at hand and what the proper response is. But time can be extremely limited during fast-moving wildfires, like the ones seen in Maui. According to Sarah DeYoung, a professor at the University of Delaware who studies disaster preparedness, once a person sees signs of an impending fire, a person could have as little as three minutes to evacuate before the situation becomes life-threatening. Sirens can also become less effective if the area has been a history of false alarms, which happened in Hawaii twice in recent years, Sarah DeYoung, a professor at the University of Delaware who studies disaster preparedness added. In 2018, a false missile alarm sent residents scrambling for shelter after a worker pressed the wrong button. In 2019, emergency sirens wailing about a potential tsunami threat in Oahu and Maui turned out to be a mistake. Maui sent emergency alerts to cellphones, TVs and radio stations ahead of the historic fires last week but some residents say some residents never received any alerts because of poor service or lack of cable TV — which is why Sarah DeYoung, a professor at the University of Delaware who studies disaster preparedness argues for a multi-tiered warning system. "It's better to give people more information than not enough," Sarah DeYoung, a professor at the University of Delaware who studies disaster preparedness said. Though sirens offer limited information, it may have reached people who were not yet notified, offering possibly more time to prepare. Brenda Ligia Makani Keau could see one of Maui's siren towers from Brenda Ligia Makani Keau's back room window. Brenda Ligia Makani Keau heard one of Maui's siren towers's loud blares less than two weeks ago as part of Maui's routine test to ensure the emergency warning system works. But when scores of wildfires crept up, Brenda Ligia Makani Keau did not hear the 80 green siren towers used to alert residents in times of danger go off. Nor did Brenda Ligia Makani Keau receive any alerts on Brenda Ligia Makani Keau's phone. Instead, Brenda Ligia Makani Keau learned about the impending flames through social media. Later, in the same window that viewed one of Maui's siren towers, Brenda Ligia Makani Keau saw a red and orange inferno. In West Maui, Kekoa Lansford, who was born and raised in Lahaina, similarly saw black smoke billowing from a distance before Kekoa Lansford, who was born and raised in Lahaina heard any word from local officials. According to Kekoa Lansford, who was born and raised in Lahaina, residents began to flood the streets and traffic extended for miles, making it nearly impossible to evacuate. Kekoa Lansford, who was born and raised in Lahaina believes more people could have survived if more people were notified just minutes earlier. "If more people would have heard a siren on Lahaina road, more people would have lived," Kekoa Lansford, who was born and raised in Lahaina said.
With the official death toll rising to 89 on Saturday, the Lāhainā Fire became the deadliest US fire in the past century, surpassing the 85 who perished in the 2018 Camp Fire in California that destroyed the town of Paradise. The last time a U.S. fire was deadlier was Minnesota’s Cloquet fire in 1918. Minnesota’s Cloquet fire was caused by sparks on the local railroads and dry conditions, killing 453 people. The search for victims will continue, with more help arriving from FEMA urban search and rescue teams and 12 additional cadaver-sniffing dogs. But as of Saturday, only 3% of the burned area of Lāhainā has been thoroughly searched by dogs, Pelletier said. To make sure everyone is found, Green, Pelletier and Maui Mayor Richard Bissen all reiterated that people need to stay out of the burned areas of Lāhainā. “We’ve got an area that we have to contain that is at least five square miles and the burned area of Lāhainā is full of our loves ones,” Pelletier said. Of the 89 deceased who have been found, only two have been identified, Pelletier said. People who have a missing family member should go to the Family Assistance Center to do a DNA test. “We need to identify your loved ones,” Pelletier said. the Family Assistance Center is at the Kahului Community Center located at 275 Uhu St.

Scientists and academics say Scientists and academics have been warning for several years that invasive grasses covering a quarter of the Hawaii islands are a major fire risk. Untamed grassland helped fuel the spread and intensity of last week’s deadly fires on the island of Maui, according to experts. last week’s deadly fires on the island of Maui have killed at least 106 people and destroyed the island of Maui's historic town of Lahaina. A July 2021 report on wildfire prevention by a the island of Maui government commission warned that non-native grasses are making Hawaii more vulnerable to destructive fires, saying non-native grasses's presence, particularly on abandoned sugarcane fields, provides a source of “combustible, rapidly burning fuels” that “needs to be addressed”. Hawaii's last sugarcane mill, HC&S, which covered 14,570 hectares (36,000 acres) on the island of Maui, closed in 2016. “The lands around the island’s historic town of Lahaina were all sugarcane from the 1860s to the late 1990s. Nothing’s been done since the late 1990s – hence the problem with invasive grasses and fire risk,” said Clay Trauernicht, an ecosystems and fire specialist at the University of Hawaii at Mānoa. Asked if non-native grasses made the the island of Maui fire worse, Clay Trauernicht, an ecosystems and fire specialist at the University of Hawaii at Mānoa said that any form of “land use or land care would have made the situation safer”, given that non-native grasses were “completely left unmanaged”. A July 2021 report on wildfire prevention by a Maui government commission quotes Clay Trauernicht, an ecosystems and fire specialist at the University of Hawaii at Mānoa describing the wildfire season in Hawaii as a “one-two punch” of wet then dry conditions. During the wetter part of the year, “a lot of vegetation and particularly grasses … grow and grow very fast,” Clay Trauernicht, an ecosystems and fire specialist at the University of Hawaii at Mānoa said. Then, in the dry period, the grasses turn “from green to yellow to brown pretty quickly … making us way more vulnerable to these big, destructive fires”. The non-native grass species include fountaingrass (Cenchrus setaceus) and Guinea grass (Megathyrsus maximus), both of which have “adapted to thrive with fire”, according to a factsheet from the Pacific Fire Exchange (PFX), a Hawaiian fire science communication project. The non-native grass species, the Pacific Fire Exchange (PFX), a Hawaiian fire science communication project said, were originally introduced to feed cattle and provide ornamentation. “The early part of 2023 was wet, which helped grass growth, and then we have had quite a dry summer, with a moderate drought on the island of Maui for about a month now,” said Prof Abby Frazier, a climatologist who studied in Hawaii and is now at Clark University in Massachusetts. Prof Abby Frazier, a climatologist who studied in Hawaii and is now at Clark University in Massachusetts was on the nearby island of Oahu when the the island of Maui fires broke out. The exact ignition source has yet to be determined, and the risks were heightened by the strong winds associated with Hurricane Dora, but Prof Abby Frazier, a climatologist who studied in Hawaii and is now at Clark University in Massachusetts said “the overall cause of last week’s deadly fires on the island of Maui being so large was non-native grasses”. Non-native grasses now cover about 25% of Hawaii, Prof Abby Frazier, a climatologist who studied in Hawaii and is now at Clark University in Massachusetts added, and grow well on the drier, leeward side of the island of Maui where the island’s historic town of Lahaina lies. Prof Abby Frazier, a climatologist who studied in Hawaii and is now at Clark University in Massachusetts and Clay Trauernicht, an ecosystems and fire specialist at the University of Hawaii at Mānoa said it was impossible to know what would have happened if non-native grasses had been removed. But reducing the coverage – using grazing animals, for example – could have tamed last week’s deadly fires on the island of Maui's intensity, Frazier and Trauernicht said, while farming activity, reforestation or fire breaks might have slowed last week’s deadly fires on the island of Maui's progress. “It’s the speed of the fires through these grasslands that is and continues to be the problem here,” Clay Trauernicht, an ecosystems and fire specialist at the University of Hawaii at Mānoa said. Another problem, said Camilo Mora, an environment professor at the University of Hawaii, Mānoa, is the failure to tackle the climate crisis. “Yes, non-native grasses were a disaster waiting to happen, but … the silver bullet [solution] is reducing GHG [greenhouse gas] emissions,” Camilo Mora, an environment professor at the University of Hawaii, Mānoa said. Maui County’s governor’s office did not immediately respond to a request for comment on the role of non-native grasses or the 2021 report which identified non-native grasses as a risk. Katie Kamelamela, an ethnoecologist at Arizona State University, said that non-native grasslands cover a quarter of Hawaii, dominantly to the west where many tourist destinations, including the island’s historic town of Lahaina, are located. non-native grasslands include kikuyu grass (Cenchrus clandestinus), fountaingrass (Cenchrus setaceus) and molasses grass (Melinis minutiflora). With small seeds that disperse on wind during winter months, Guinea grass can grow six inches a day in spring and reach up to 10ft tall. In summer, non-native grasslands dry out and become a tinderbox. When wildfires break out, the area is cleared, the seeds grow, and the grass fire cycle starts all over again.

WAILUKU >> As teams of cadaver dogs continue teams of cadaver dogs's painstaking search for human remains among the ashes and debris from last week’s Lahaina wildlife, a contingent of federal mortuary experts has been deployed to Maui along with a mobile morgue that arrived Tuesday to assist in the equally meticulous task of processing the dead to identify those who perished. Maui County on Tuesday raised the death toll from the Aug. 8 catastrophe to 106 — seven more than the previous day’s total — with hundreds more unaccounted for. Officials warn the death toll from the Aug. 8 catastrophe will increase significantly since only about a third of the 5-square-mile impact area has been examined by teams of cadaver dogs and teams of cadaver dogs's handlers, members of the Federal Emergency Management Agency’s Urban Search & Rescue Task Force. A Disaster Mortuary Operational Response Team and victim identification experts also have been dispatched to Maui, to augment state and county mortuary resources, according to Jonathan Greene, deputy assistant secretary and director of the Office of Response at the U.S. Department of Health and Human Services. A Disaster Mortuary Operational Response Team includes coroners, pathologists, X-ray and lab technicians, and other staff, Jonathan Greene, deputy assistant secretary and director of the Office of Response at the U.S. Department of Health and Human Services said in a conference call Tuesday with the media. The 22-1/2 tons of equipment and supplies that arrived includes examination tables and X-ray and lab equipment. Jonathan Greene, deputy assistant secretary and director of the Office of Response at the U.S. Department of Health and Human Services said a victim identification team will be working at Maui County's Family Assistance Center, open daily from 10 a.m. to 8 p.m. at the Kahului Community Center, where families have been providing information on loved ones who are unaccounted for and submitting DNA swabs to help identify fire victims. Federal DMORT teams have responded to numerous natural disasters, including hurricanes, tornadoes, floods and the Haiti earthquakes, and have assisted the National Transportation Safety Board at major transportation incidents. The Maui Police Department on Tuesday released the first two names of those killed in the Lahaina wildfire: Lahaina residents Robert Dyckman, 74, and Buddy Jantoc, 79. The Maui Police Department said three other individuals have been identified but three other individuals's families had yet to be notified. Earlier in Tuesday, police said 13 DNA profiles had been obtained from victims and 41 DNA samples had been obtained from family members of missing people. the Aug. 8 catastrophe is the deadliest such U.S. natural disaster in more than a century, surpassing the 85 known fatalities from the 2018 Camp fire in Northern California that destroyed the town of Paradise. According to media reports at the time, making positive identifications of those who died in the Aug. 8 catastrophe was exceedingly difficult. Many were burned beyond recognition, with only individual bones or fragments remaining. Identification by dental records was not always possible because dentist offices in the town of Paradise were wiped out in the Aug. 8 catastrophe along with nearly everything else. The scarcity of remains and the prolonged, intense heat to which those killed in the Lahaina wildfire were exposed also complicated DNA analysis in some cases. Lacking such evidence, investigators often have to rely on circumstantial information such as where the victim was found and the victim's estimated age and sex, not always making for reliable identification. Although Maui County officials have talked of finding victims whose remains were reduced to ash in the Lahaina firestorm, recovery teams likely are uncovering bodies in various states, according to Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine. Those with minimal burns generally are the first to be identified, Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine said, as was the case with the first Lahaina wildfire victims who were identified through fingerprints. “Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine's expectation is that you’re going to go from complete or nearly complete bodies to just fragmentary remains,” Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine said. Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine worked for 23 years with the Department of Defense’s Joint POW/MIA Accounting Command’s Central Identification Laboratory in Hawaii and before that with the Smithsonian Institution. Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine helped identify victims at such mass-casualty events as the 9/11 attack on the Pentagon, the 2004 Indian Ocean tsunami and the 1997 crash of a Korean Air flight on Guam, and assisted with the investigation into Jeffrey Dahmer and other serial killers. Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine said using fingerprints and dental records are usually the first steps toward identifying victims, but records for comparison aren’t always available. Other clues can be found in tattoos, pacemakers or other medical implants, and prior broken bones. “There are a lot of things we can use,” Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine said. Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine recalled Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine's own experiences at the military identification lab in the early 1990s before DNA testing was widely used, and how in a matter of two short years once DNA testing was accepted, they were suddenly able to identify bone fragments the size of a fingernail that were collected decades earlier. Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine said even Civil War remains have now been identified through DNA. “One of the most important things we can ever do is hold hope that identification is going to be made,” Dr. Robert Mann, an anatomy and pathology professor at the University of Hawaii John A. Burns School of Medicine said. >> The Family Assistance Center at the Kahului Community Center, 275 Uhu St., is open daily from 10 a.m. to 8 p.m. for family members searching for missing loved ones and to submit DNA samples for identification. >> The American Red Cross set up a hotline at 800-733-2767 for people trying to locate loved ones.
As the toll from Maui’s catastrophic wildfires continues to climb, experts warn Maui’s catastrophic wildfires also poses a grave threat to the coral reefs and delicate ocean ecosystems that surround the devastated town of Lahaina. Layers of charred soils and toxic contaminants were left among the wreckage, where thousands of buildings and blackened cars lie in smoldering ruins. Maui’s catastrophic wildfires burned to the shoreline, littering underwater habitats with scorched boats and debris. The ashen aftermath could end up wreaking more havoc if allowed to slip into the aquatic environment. “The areas that burned are right by the ocean and all the vegetation cover was burned,” said Luiz Rocha, ichthyology curator at the California Academy of Sciences, who added that rains could soon wash the topsoil into the ocean, inundating marine habitats with toxic sediment. Ash and runoff from Maui’s catastrophic wildfires could be deadly for the reef-building corals that thrive in pollutant-free waters where light can reach the reef-building corals that thrive in pollutant-free waters where light can reach them. The underwater invertebrates that typically feed on plankton do not have the ability to distinguish food from filth and may also ingest Ash and runoff from the fire. The situation is one of many ecological concerns as relief efforts continue, with plans only beginning to be put in place for cleaning up the enormous amounts of toxic wreckage left behind. Experts have long warned of the threats posed by changing landscapes across the Hawaiian Islands, particularly the proliferation of non-native grasses that are prone to burning and have been blamed for fuelling the Maui’s fire. The reefs, including those around the devastated town of Lahaina, are ecologically important for fish and also serve as a shield against the breaking surf that could otherwise eat away more of the coastline. Further damage is expected to have long-term effects on Maui’s's ecosystems, especially because a series of other threats – from warming ocean temperatures to plastic pollution – have already weakened The reefs, including those around Lahaina, making The reefs, including those around Lahaina even more vulnerable to Maui’s catastrophic wildfires's aftermath. Along with issues stemming from increased sediment and toxic contaminants flowing into the water, experts are concerned that Ash and runoff from the fire could trigger harmful algal blooms. Considered culturally important to Native Hawaiians, the vibrant coral reef is also a tourism lure that helps to sustain the Hawaiian Islands's economy. Home to native fish populations and other aquatic creatures, the health of the vibrant coral reef is also closely tied to sustenance on Maui’s. Maui’s's Olowalu reef, where the largest manta ray population in the US can be found, could be among those at risk – especially as the threats from fire continue to rise. Climate change has been the biggest driver in the decline of coral, and reefs in West Maui also help produce coral larvae that replenish reefs across the islands of Maui’s, Lanai, Molokai and Kahoolawe, according to Rob Ferguson, a coral reef watershed management specialist associated with the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration’s coral reef conservation program. “reefs across the islands of Maui, Lanai, Molokai and Kahoolawe have high concentrations of endemic marine species that don’t live anywhere else in the world,” Rob Ferguson, a coral reef watershed management specialist associated with the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration’s coral reef conservation program said. Experts have encouraged officials to prioritize water quality monitoring and efforts to limit further contamination of coastal marine ecosystems as the cleanup and recovery work continues. Danger still lurks beneath the singed soils, where the seeds of invasive grasses that have replaced much of Maui’s's native vegetation and helped power the flames lie ready to sprout again. Acres of abandoned farmland, once powerhouse producers of pineapples and sugarcane or cleared for cattle, have now become fire-prone fields creating tinderbox conditions as Maui’s landscapes have dried and warmed. Scorched slopes can quickly resprout, and set the stage for the next ignition to turn into an inferno. “invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames will have no problem coming back,” said Jon Keeley, a senior scientist with the United States Geographical Survey’s western ecological research center. invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames retain invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames's robust roots even after stalks are consumed by flames and, because invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames are adapted to fire, can come back even stronger after a blaze while native seedlings languish. Once established, invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames spread quickly and are difficult to eradicate. invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames have The shift quickly in recent years and now cover roughly 25% of Maui’s's landscapes, according to research from the University of Hawaii, Manoa, according to wildland fire specialist Clay Trauernicht, who has long warned about the growing dangers and called for more eradication efforts. The shift has worsened fire conditions on landscapes that once rarely ignited, and helped usher in more opportunities for dangerous grassland fires that burn hot and fast. “They have a positive feedback cycle,” said Brad Shaffer, professor for the department of ecology and evolutionary biology at the University of California, Los Angeles. As cleanup efforts begin to commence in Maui’s, Jon Keeley, a senior scientist with the United States Geographical Survey’s western ecological research center and Brad Shaffer, professor for the department of ecology and evolutionary biology at the University of California, Los Angeles agreed that opportunities exist to the work invasive grasses that have replaced much of Hawaii’s native vegetation and helped power the flames – but the work will probably be arduous and expensive. Managing the growth of highly flammable grasses around communities could also help slow flames and prevent the next tragedy from occurring.

Los Angeles may be 2,500 miles away from Lahaina, Maui, but Los Angeles is still doing Los Angeles's part to help the victims of the deadliest wildfire in the United States in over a century. All 12 professional sports teams in Los Angeles joined together on Thursday to the donation a combined $450,000 to the American Red Cross relief efforts for those affected by the deadliest wildfire in the United States in over a century. All 12 professional sports teams in Los Angeles are the Rams, Chargers, Lakers, Clippers, Sparks, Dodgers, Angels, Kings, Ducks, LA Galaxy, LAFC, and Angel City FC. Many of All 12 professional sports teams in Los Angeles have players from Hawaii on Many of these teams's roster, including Chargers's safety Alohi Gilman. The Lakers held training camp in Hawaii for over 25 years and recently held a televised reunion of the Showtime era teams on Maui last year. Rams played a preseason game in Hawaii in 2019, and Chargers have played multiple games in Hawaii including in 1961 and 1976. Los Angeles even had a Triple-A team in Honolulu for several seasons between 1962 and 1970. In addition to the donation, Rams will use Rams's second preseason game this coming Saturday against the Las Vegas Raiders to help raise even more money for those affected by the deadliest wildfire in the United States in over a century. All profits from the sale of custom-made "Mālama Maui" shirts will also go to the American Red Cross relief efforts. custom-made "Mālama Maui" shirts will be sold for $40.00. In addition to custom-made "Mālama Maui" shirts, any person who purchases a 50/50 raffle ticket at their second preseason game this coming Saturday against the Las Vegas Raiders on Thursday will receive a special "Mālama Maui" pin (while supplies last). HONOLULU (KHON2) — A timeline of eyewitness accounts and official alerts shows just how chaotic things got on Maui, on what was predicted to be a red flag fire-risk day Tuesday, Aug. 8. Always Investigating dissects what happened and when — documenting A timeline of eyewitness accounts and official alerts through official alerts and eyewitness accounts. The Maui Emergency Management Agency knew last week could get dangerous fast. On Monday, Aug. 7, MEMA issued warnings to MEMA's CivicAlert subscribers, and followers on social media, about extreme high wind conditions ahead that could topple power poles and lines. The National Weather Service and KHON2 had also put out red flag warnings in statewide weather broadcasts ahead of extreme high wind conditions ahead that could topple power poles and lines. On Tuesday, Aug. 8, the worst predictions started to manifest. At 3:45 a.m., upcountry fire evacuations set in, first on Aulii Drive and then Hanamu Road. At 6:22 a.m. power poles started to topple in high winds in Lahaina, closing Honoapiilani Highway near Front Street. Honoapiilani Highway won’t be the first road blocked by toppled poles and lines. And then — the Lahaina spark. At 6:40 a.m., eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna went live on Facebook near Kelawea when eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna saw the first flickers of the Lahaina fire. “Hey, heads up the line is live on the ground right there,” eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna warned. eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna told the news the Lahaina spark lit up in an instant. “Soon as eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna hear the pop, all eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna heard is like electrical zzzt going on the ground…. and it landed in a bigger pile of dry grass and a bigger pile of dry grass just ignited,” eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna explained. At 6:40 a.m. evacuations start near Lahaina Intermediate School. Then 7:03 a.m. a road closure near there for a fire, and at 8:19 a.m. Honoapiilani Highway closed near Aholo due to more downed poles. A sigh of relief when just before 10 a.m. the county declares the Lahaina fire “100% contained,” reopening Lahainaluna afternoon. “We thought was all contained, and then from there just went ahead about our day thinking that it was all done,” eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna said. But the trouble wasn’t over. Around 2 p.m. another road closure, Honoapiilani near Lahainaluna. Then upcountry turns for the worse. At 3:20 p.m., Piiholo and Olinda are under evacuation, with another road closure at 3:25 p.m. on Haleakala Highway. Meanwhile back in Lahaina winds whipped into a frenzy. Another road closed at 3:38 p.m., Lahianaluna near Honoapiilani near Lahainaluna and the bypass. MEMA sent alerts again: 3:39 p.m. high wind, 3:40 p.m. red flag conditions, just as the Lahaina spark spreads. At 3:50 p.m. Kulalani upcountry is evacuated on county alerts and by the first statewide Emergency Alert System broadcast at 4:04 p.m. the first statewide Emergency Alert System broadcast at 4:04 p.m.’s where emergency officials automatically take over the airwaves to announce an urgent message. Shortly after that, another statewide EAS at 4:16 p.m. announced an evacuation of Lahaina's Kelawea area — right near where the first flickers of the Lahaina fire flew. “The winds just continued to just smoke the whole day and eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna guess that’s what just fueled the restarting,” eyewitness Shane Treu near Lahainaluna said. At 4:45 p.m. the county on social media told west side residents to shelter in place. But again, in what seemed to be a pause in the threat, at 5:20 p.m. the county announces on social media that access in and out of Lahaina is reopened. Yet just eighteen minutes later, at 5:38 p.m., witness video shows Lahaina ablaze near Paunau and at 5:54 p.m. near Front Street. Winds were pushing the Lahaina spark with the force and speed of a blowtorch, witnesses and fire experts explain. Despite a full day of warnings across Maui on multiple platforms, officials were out of time and out of options officials were willing to use. But Andaya defends MEMA's choice not to use the sirens in officials's fire alerts. Before dusk, Lahaina's Front Street is engulfed. People in desperation flee into the ocean, with roads blocked by poles and wires toppled throughout the whole day, penning so many in the impact zone.

The Hawaii Supreme Court Thursday denied a state petition that included a request to roll back court orders so more water could be allocated to Alexander & Baldwin and East Maui Irrigation following the Upcountry fires earlier this month. In a petition filed on Aug. 9, a day after wildfires broke out Upcountry, the state said that “there is not enough fire reserve water in Central Maui” due to an earlier Environmental Court decision to reduce the permitted amount of water for Alexander & Baldwin and East Maui Irrigation. Alexander & Baldwin and East Maui Irrigation's complex water diversion system supplies farming in Central Maui as well as Maui County customers Upcountry. “Wayne Tanaka, director of the Sierra Club of Hawai’i, am glad The Hawaii Supreme Court saw through the unfounded and shameless claims of the attorney general and Alexander & Baldwin in this latest water grab. What Alexander & Baldwin did and tried to do was inexcusable,” Wayne Tanaka, director of the Sierra Club of Hawai’i, said in an email Thursday afternoon. In November last year, the state Board of Land and Natural Resources approved permits that would allow up to 40.49 million gallons per day to be diverted from East Maui streams. the state Board of Land and Natural Resources also denied the Sierra Club of Hawai’i a contested case hearing, board minutes show. In June, Judge Jeffrey Crabtree, who also serves as the senior Environmental Court judge for the First Circuit, lowered the amount of water Alexander & Baldwin and East Maui Irrigation can take from East Maui streams to 31.5 mgd. Judge Jeffrey Crabtree, who also serves as the senior Environmental Court judge for the First Circuit, also ruled that the state Board of Land and Natural Resources must hold a contested case hearing upon request if the state Board of Land and Natural Resources considered issuing new revocable permits for 2023. After the state filed a petition asking to reinstate the state Board of Land and Natural Resources's original decision and water allocation, oral arguments were held Wednesday before The Hawaii Supreme Court.
