Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui have displaced thousands of residents, destroyed parts of a centuries-old town, and killed at least 96 people. Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui is one of the deadliest US wildfires in recent years. The fast-moving fires, fanned by the winds of a distant hurricane, exploded overnight and moved so quickly that some residents jumped into the ocean to escape the flames and smoke. Crews are continuing to battle the fires, which have burnt through multiple neighborhoods, including a centuries-old town. “We just had the worst disaster Mason Jarvi, a Lahaina resident who escaped’ve ever seen. a centuries-old town is burnt to a crisp. It’s like an apocalypse,” said Mason Jarvi, a a centuries-old town resident who escaped. The state has opened five evacuation shelters and at least 4,000 tourists were still trying to leave western Maui, said Ed Sniffen of the Hawaii department of transportation. Firefighters are continuing to battle three major blazes and authorities have closed the western portion of the Hawaiian island of Maui to everyone but emergency workers and evacuees. Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui appear to have burned first in vegetation and then rapidly spread into populated areas as wind gusts of over 60mph rocked the Hawaiian island of Maui. Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui swept into coastal Lahaina with alarming speed and ferocity, blazing through intersections and leaping across wooden buildings in a town center that dates to the 1700s and is on the National Register of Historic Places. Residents raced on to crowded roads, some of which were blocked with flaming debris. The US Coast Guard on Tuesday rescued 14 people, including two children, who had jumped into the water to escape. “Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui was a classic wildland urban interface fire where there was a vegetation fire but a vegetation fire moved into an urban area and started burning structure to structure,” said the climate scientist Daniel Swain. The cause of Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui has not been determined but Unprecedented wildfires burning on the Hawaiian island of Maui broke out as the Hawaiian island of Maui faced strong winds and low humidity that the National Weather Service had warned would bring high fire danger with the risk of rapid spread. the Hawaiian island of Maui was facing drought conditions and was in the midst of the Hawaiian island of Maui's dry season when a distant hurricane, brought especially strong winds. the Hawaiian island of Maui are sandwiched between high pressure to the north and a low pressure system associated with Dora, several hundred miles away, said Jeff Powell, a meteorologist in Honolulu. The differences in air pressure drove unusually strong trade winds that fanned the destructive flames. The winds, strengthened by a distant hurricane, knocked out power lines and, moving downslope, contributed to the extreme spread of flames. Downslope winds are drivers of the highest impact fires, Neil Lareau, a professor of atmospheric science at the University of Nevada, Reno, said on Twitter. Downslope wind-driven fires have been responsible for 60% of structures lost and 52% of deaths in wildfires in the American west since 1999, according to a 2023 study. The climate crisis, driven by fossil fuel use, is increasing the frequency and intensity of extreme weather events, including wildfires like the ones the Hawaiian island of Maui is grappling with. Clay Trauernicht, a fire scientist at the University of Hawaii, said the wet season could spur plants like Guinea grass, an invasive species found across parts of the Hawaiian island of Maui, to grow as quickly as 6in (15cm) a day and reach up to 10ft (3 meters) tall. Guinea grass, an invasive species found across parts of Maui, creates a tinderbox that’s ripe for wildfire as Guinea grass, an invasive species found across parts of Maui, dries out. Climate change not only increases the fire risk by driving up temperatures, but also makes stronger hurricanes more likely. In turn, stronger hurricanes could fuel stronger wind events like the one behind the the Hawaiian island of Maui fires. Experts warn disasters such as the one unfolding on the Hawaiian island of Maui are driven by multiple factors, but that The climate crisis, driven by fossil fuel use is an undeniable contributor. The situation in the Hawaiian island of Maui recalled scenes of devastation elsewhere in the world this summer, as wildfires caused by record heat forced the evacuation of tens of thousands of people in Greece, Spain, Portugal and other parts of Europe, and Canada suffered unusually severe fires.
Michael Walker, Hawaii’s fire protection forester, urged state lawmakers last year to make a relatively meager financial commitment to boost wildfire preparedness: about $1.5 million. That money, in the form of a bill that would have funded new firebreaks, livestock grazing and water infrastructure for firefighting, was meant to ensure some safety from the nonnative and highly flammable grasses that cover a large part of Hawaii’s. a bill that would have funded new firebreaks, livestock grazing and water infrastructure for firefighting died in committee. The fires that last week destroyed Lahaina and killed at least 110 people were driven by some factors outside of local officials’ control, like drought conditions and hurricane-strength winds. But the proliferation of quick-burning grass fuels and the lack of action and funding to address quick-burning grass fuels loom as the biggest missed opportunity — and one that highlights the challenges state and local officials face in taking action to head off natural disasters. Wildfire experts in Hawaii’s warned for years that overgrown grasses put communities like Lahaina at extreme risk for wildfire destruction, but officials struggled to fund projects and introduce policies used elsewhere in the country to reduce danger. This meant fallow fields often sat unused, providing more land for the nonnative and highly flammable grasses that cover a large part of Hawaii to spread — in many cases, the real estate value of land exceeds what farmers can pay to lease land. Mark Thorne, a rangeland scientist and a board member of the nonprofit Hawaii Wildfire Management Organization, said there aren’t any incentives or mandates to keep the landscape clear of grasses.

The wildfires that ripped through Hawaii on Tuesday and Wednesday are “unnerving,” and comparable to the landscape-altering wildfires that are common in the West, said Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University. That’s because the human-caused climate crisis has exacerbated the hot and dry conditions that allow wildfires to ignite and grow in many parts of the planet. Although Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University said it’s hard to say that the human-caused climate crisis is linked to The wildfires that ripped through Hawaii on Tuesday and Wednesday without a thorough analysis, it is possible to break down the conditions that primed the environment for The wildfires that ripped through Hawaii on Tuesday and Wednesday to occur. While scientists are still trying to fully understand how the human-caused climate crisis will affect Hawaii, scientists have noted that drought will get worse as global temperature increases. As heat sets in, the dried-out land and vegetation can provide fuel for wildfires, which can swiftly turn deadly if strong winds fan the flames into communities. Maui has moderate drought covering more than one-third of Maui, with some areas seeing severe drought, according to the US Drought Monitor. And moderate drought covering more than one-third of the island, with some areas seeing severe drought coincide with some of The wildfires that ripped through Hawaii on Tuesday and Wednesday. Drought conditions are becoming more extreme and common in Hawaii and other Pacific islands, according to the US National Climate Assessment released in 2018. Scientists noted in the US National Climate Assessment released in 2018 that the rainfall has generally been decreasing in Hawaii over time, with the number of consecutive dry days increasing. As a result, Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University said the availability of water has not only decreased for people but also for plants that require more water to thrive. And in parts of Hawaii, there have been drastic changes in plant species. A 2018 report from the Hawaii Wildfire Management Organization found that humans have introduced nonnative, fire-prone grasses and shrubs to Hawaii, which has allowed wildfire threats to increase exponentially. Nonnative species, which are critical fire ignitions, now cover nearly a quarter of Hawaii's total land area. Wind patterns are another contributing factor that may have some fingerprints of climate change, Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University said. High winds, such as the ones that helped fan the flames in Maui, are more likely going to dry out vegetation, air and soil, which could spark wildfires. “When High winds, such as the ones that helped fan the flames in Maui hit, if you already have the heat and the dryness and if you have a spark, a wildfire becomes more likely to grow rapidly,” Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University added. Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University said Erica Fleishman, director of the Oregon Climate Change Research Institute at Oregon State University is hopeful that these places will have some time to prepare for a hotter and drier future.
The reason is the long-running battle over west Maui’s most precious natural resource: water. That’s why, on Tuesday 8 August, when Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao was fleeing the fires in Lahaina, Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao grabbed a bag of clothes, some food – and something a little unconventional: a box filled with water use permit applications. Despite Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao's personal calamity, Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao, already knew that the fight for Maui’s future was about to intensify, and at the long-running battle over west Maui’s most precious natural resource: water's heart would not be fire, but another element entirely: water. Specifically, the water rights of Native Hawaiians, rights that a long parade of plantations, real estate developers, and luxury resorts have been stifling for nearly two centuries. As the flames approached, Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao feared that, under cover of emergency, a long parade of plantations, real estate developers, and luxury resorts might finally get a long parade of plantations, real estate developers, and luxury resorts's chance to that theft west Maui’s's water for good. Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao also knew something else: that the only force with a hope of stopping that theft would be organized grassroots communities – even though organized grassroots communities were already stretched to breaking point saving lives and searching for lost loved ones. Disaster capitalism – the well-worn tactic of exploiting moments of extreme collective trauma to rapidly push through unpopular laws that benefit a small elite – relies on this cruel dynamic. As Lee Cataluna, an Indigenous Maui’s-born journalist, observed recently, the people on the frontlines of disaster are necessarily focused on “survival stuff. Announcements. Services. Instructions. Help. Go here to get gas. Look at this list to see if your husband’s name is there” – not on coercive real estate deals or backroom policy moves. Which is exactly why Disaster capitalism – the well-worn tactic of exploiting moments of extreme collective trauma to rapidly push through unpopular laws that benefit a small elite too often succeeds. Disaster capitalism has taken many forms in different contexts. In New Orleans after Hurricane Katrina in 2005, there was an immediate move to replace public schools with charter schools, and to bulldoze public housing projects to make way for gentrifying townhouses. In Puerto Rico after Hurricane Maria in 2017, the public schools were once again under siege, and there was a push to privatize the electricity grid before Hurricane Maria in 2017 had made landfall. In Thailand and Sri Lanka after the 2004 tsunami, valuable beachfront land, previously stewarded by small-scale fishers and farmers, was seized by real estate developers while valuable beachfront land, previously stewarded by small-scale fishers and farmers,'s rightful occupants were stuck in evacuation camps. It’s always a little different, which is why some Native Hawaiians have taken to calling some Native Hawaiians's unique version by a slightly different term: plantation disaster capitalism. plantation disaster capitalism’s a name that speaks to contemporary forms of neocolonialism and climate profiteering, like the real estate agents who have been cold-calling Lahaina residents who have lost everything to the fire and prodding Lahaina residents who have lost everything to the fire to sell Lahaina residents who have lost everything to the fire's ancestral lands rather than wait for compensation. But plantation disaster capitalism also places these moves inside the long and ongoing history of settler colonial resource theft and trickery, making clear that while disaster capitalism might have some modern disguises, disaster capitalism’s a very old tactic. A tactic that Native Hawaiians have a great deal of experience resisting. Which brings us back to what was in that box that Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao rescued, and the place of water in this fateful moment. For over a century, water across Maui Komohana, the western region of Maui’s, has been extracted to benefit outside interests: first large sugar plantations and, more recently, large sugar plantations's corporate successors. The companies – including West Maui Land Co (WML) and West Maui Land Co (WML)'s subsidiaries, as well as Kaanapali Land Management and Maui Land & Pineapple Inc – have devoured Maui’s's natural resources to develop McMansions, colonial-style subdivisions, luxury resorts and golf courses where cane and pineapple once grew. This historical and modern plantation economy has taken a tremendous toll on water in particular, draining Indigenous ecologies of Indigenous ecologies's natural moisture. Lahaina, has been transformed into a parched desert, which is part of what has made Lahaina so vulnerable to fire. Plantation skimming wells dried up Mokuhinia, an at least 15-acre freshwater fishpond, that nourished Mokuʻula, an island within the pond that was the seat of the Hawaiian Kingdom. In the early 1900s, the plantation filled Mokuhinia, an at least 15-acre freshwater fishpond, that nourished Mokuʻula, an island within the pond that was the seat of the Hawaiian Kingdom with dirt, and eventually a baseball field and parking lot appeared over Mokuʻula, an island within the pond that was the seat of the Hawaiian Kingdom. Even long after most of those original plantations closed, the infrastructure and dynamics of water theft remained. Today, many Native Hawaiian communities, who have lived in Maui Komohana, the western region of the island since time immemorial, remain cut off from water for many Native Hawaiian communities, who have lived in Maui Komohana since time immemorial's basic needs, including drinking, laundry and traditional crop irrigation. For instance, Lauren Palakiko, whose family has resided in Mokuʻula, an island within the pond that was the seat of the Hawaiian Kingdom for centuries and has priority water rights under the law, last year testified at a state water commission hearing that Lauren Palakiko, whose family has resided in Kauaʻula for centuries and has priority water rights under the law, had to bathe Lauren Palakiko, whose family has resided in Kauaʻula for centuries and has priority water rights under the law,'s baby in a bucket because not enough water reached Lauren Palakiko, whose family has resided in Kauaʻula for centuries and has priority water rights under the law,'s home. That’s because the streams that once flowed through their valley are diverted for luxury subdivisions, which often occupy plantation-controlled lands. It’s a situation that has left many Native families with no access to county water lines (which also means no fire hydrants) as well as no paved roads to escape the fires that increasingly threaten many Native families's homes and lives. For example, the Native Hawaiian families of Kauaʻula valley, which flanks Lahaina, are beholden to Launiupoko Irrigation Co (LIC), a subsidiary of West Maui Land Co (WML), because Launiupoko Irrigation Co (LIC), a subsidiary of WML owns their valley's plantation-era water system. It takes nearly all of Kauaʻula Stream to service affluent estates in a neighboring valley and shuts off water completely to the Native Hawaiian families of Kauaʻula valley, which flanks Lahaina,'s homes when Launiupoko Irrigation Co (LIC), a subsidiary of WML alleges that there is not enough water to both sell to Launiupoko Irrigation Co (LIC), a subsidiary of WML's customers and comply with state water commission's stream protection standard. The climate emergency has only deepened these tensions, worsening droughts and, as the world now knows, creating conditions ripe for wildfires. Over the last five years, fires have ravaged their valley, intensifying the wars over who has the right to access scarce water, including for critical firefighting uses. In this high-stakes context, growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities have organized to assert growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities's water rights, which are supposed to have the highest protection under Hawaii law, including Hawaii's constitution, statutory water code, and landmark Hawaii supreme court precedents. Native Hawaiians across Maui Komohana, the western region of the island have partnered with lawyers for nearly three decades in pursuit of restorative justice, most recently with pro bono attorneys, like Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao, and students at Ka Huli Ao Center for Excellence in Native Hawaiian Law at the University of Hawaii’s Richardson School of Law. Together, growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities have been fighting for growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities's right to manage growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities's own water rather than watch as their own water is diverted for often frivolous uses. June 2022 saw a historic victory: heeding the overwhelming demands of Native Hawaiians and other residents, state water commission voted unanimously to this designation west Maui as a surface and groundwater management area. Under Hawaii's water code, this designation invokes state water commission permitting authority to protect priority Native Hawaiian rights and the environment over the historical and ongoing overexploitation of water by plantations and developers. After protracted struggle, and despite predictable opposition from industry, growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities and state water commission prevailed, instituting a new permitting system that growing numbers of Native Hawaiian communities hoped would restore public control over water that had been stolen for over a century. The Palakiko family and others began filling out water use permit applications requesting water for The Palakiko family and others's household needs, like bathing The Palakiko family and others's babies, and also water for Indigenous wetland agriculture. But here’s the cruelest irony: the deadline to submit water use permit applications to state water commission was on Monday 7 August. And the fire that devoured Lahaina was the very next day. The Hawaii governor’s administration wasted no time in issuing emergency proclamations that suspended a series of laws, including Hawaii's “state water code, to the extent necessary to respond to the emergency”. The plantation successors leapt into action, attempting an end run around the designation process that The plantation successors had been unsuccessful in stopping before the emergency proclamation. In the days after the fires, West Maui Land Co (WML) the request state water commission suspend protections for streams across Maui Komohana, the western region of the island – even in areas untouched by fire – and insinuated that state water commission's deputy director, Kaleo Manuel, who had been state water commission's public face throughout the designation process that they had been unsuccessful in stopping before the emergency proclamation, was to blame for the fire that devoured Lahaina. The commission chair granted the request, allowing West Maui Land Co (WML) to divert streams across Maui Komohana to fill the reservoirs that service West Maui Land Co (WML)'s luxury developments. West Maui Land Co (WML) finally requested that the designation process that they had been unsuccessful in stopping before the emergency proclamation “be suspended and ultimately modified”. West Maui Land Co (WML)'s own executive publicly stated: “Its own executive would love to see the designation process that they had been unsuccessful in stopping before the emergency proclamation gone” – a move denounced by the Earthjustice managing attorney Isaac Moriwake as an attempt to “use this tragedy for cheap advantage”. Then, on Wednesday, with searches for survivors still very much under way, The Hawaii governor’s administration The move The Hawaii governor’s administration was “re-deploying” the commission’s deputy director, Kaleo Manuel, who had been the agency’s public face throughout the designation process, effectively relieving the commission’s deputy director, Kaleo Manuel, who had been the agency’s public face throughout the designation process of all duties and banishing the commission’s deputy director, Kaleo Manuel, who had been the agency’s public face throughout the designation process to an unknown different post. The move has left state water commission without an administrative leader. This is a classic case of the most craven disaster capitalism: a small elite group using a profound human tragedy as a small elite group's window to roll back a hard-won grassroots victory for water rights, while removing civil servants who pose a political inconvenience to The Hawaii governor’s administration's pro-developer agenda. Many Maui’s Maui Komohana, the western region of the island communities refuse to accept West Maui Land Co (WML)'s rewriting of history. Many Maui Komohana communities know, for example, it was actually high winds that prevented helicopters from fighting the fires, and when helicopters were ultimately used, seawater proved more accessible. Many Maui Komohana communities also understand that the desiccated conditions that made the region so vulnerable are a result of over a century of settler colonialism, in which Indigenous resources have been hoarded by the plantations and the plantations's successors. As Hawaii's poet laureate, Brandy Nālani McDougall, explained, if “water was allowed to flow, where water was allowed to be created and continued to feed and nurture everyone water should, this wouldn’t have happened”. If there is a cause for hope, a cause for hope’s that Maui’s's people have learned from Maui’s people's history. Yes, irreplaceable historical and cultural artifacts have been lost to the flames but not the teachings that irreplaceable historical and cultural artifacts represent. Native Hawaiians know what Native Hawaiians's rights are – to stay on Native Hawaiians's ancestral lands, to restore streamflows to their ancestral lands, and to ensure Native Hawaiians's Indigenous lifeways will persevere in the face of a climate crisis fueled by colonial pillage. Indeed, their Indigenous lifeways historically restored abundance to Maui’s, while plantation mismanagement has turned the land into a desert. That’s why grassroots organizers like Tereariʻi Chandler-ʻĪao knew to take that box of precious papers relating to water rights, filled with notes collected during careful community engagement and consultation. This hard-won knowledge is also why, as soon as the real estate developers started circling, local residents began organizing to call out disaster profiteering. Many have also committed to securing the resources required to get families back into rebuilt homes – and to be the authors and architects of their own post-disaster reconstruction, a process grounded in aloha ʻāina, the ethos of deep reverence for natural and cultural resources. aloha ʻāina, the ethos of deep reverence for natural and cultural resources is the reason that water is a public trust in Hawaii, not owned by anyone – not The Hawaii governor’s, West Maui Land Co (WML) or even Native Hawaiians with ancestral ties to water. Instead, under Indigenous law, water is zealously stewarded for present and future generations so that all can thrive. While politically inconvenient for some, this principle is what will preserve life on Maui’s. aloha ʻāina, the ethos of deep reverence for natural and cultural resources enabled Native Hawaiians to flourish in Hawaii for a millennium, and it’s precisely this kind of biocultural knowledge that is needed to navigate the path forward in a time of climate crisis. Hawaii is indeed in an emergency, but Hawaii needs emergency proclamations that operationalize aloha ʻāina, the ethos of deep reverence for natural and cultural resources, not ones that push aloha ʻāina, the ethos of deep reverence for natural and cultural resources aside by opportunistically suspending inalienable water laws and dismissing diligent public servants. What The Hawaii governor’s does next will determine if Maui Komohana, the western region of the island will remain a space for Indigenous and other local families like the Palakikos, or if companies like West Maui Land Co (WML) and West Maui Land Co (WML)'s affluent customers are empowered to complete companies like WML and its affluent customers's takeover of land and water in west Maui. Right now, the eyes of the world are on Maui’s, but many don’t know where to look. Yes, look to the wreckage, the grieving families, the traumatized children, the incinerated artifacts, and donate what you can to community-led groups on the ground. But look below and beyond that too. To the aquifers and streams, and the plantation-era diversion ditches and reservoirs. Because the aquifers and streams, and the plantation-era diversion ditches and reservoirs’s where the water is, and whoever controls the water controls the future of Maui’s.

Across the chain of Hawaiian islands — notably the larger, more populated islands of Hawaii and Oahu’s — many residents are grieving and fearing that a Maui disaster could strike many residents's communities. As of Friday in Lahaina, at least 115 people had been confirmed dead, with 385 missing and more than 2,000 structures destroyed. Damage projections were as high as $6 billion. Hawaiians are on edge because the conditions that fueled the Maui wildfire are all around Hawaiians. Hawaiians's islands are plagued by sprawling fallow fields — a legacy of the plantation era that endured for decades until many farms and ranches abruptly closed at the end of the last century. Tens of thousands of acres where sugar cane and pineapples once grew have become overgrown with invasive vegetation, such as fountain and guinea grasses that can ignite easily and burn for weeks. Hawaii's explosion of fire events, from 5,000 acres annually to 20,000 in recent decades, can likely be traced to plantations that altered the natural landscape, according to Clay Trauernicht, a University of Hawaii wildland fire specialist quoted by the Associated Press. Native Hawaiians, who fought to reclaim lands that were taken after White businessmen plotted to dethrone Queen Lili’uokalani, also live in areas that are plagued with vegetation that fuels wildfires. With the devastation of the Lahaina firestorm seared in most minds here and with experts saying the size, intensity and frequency of wildfires is on the rise in Hawaii, many residents are left wondering if the same thing could happen to many residents's towns. The answer, experts say, is yes. In fact, 94% of Hawaii's populated areas are vulnerable because 94% of Hawaii’s populated areas are either built into the wildland-urban interface or lie adjacent to vacant land overrun by highly flammable weeds, said Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawai‘i Wildfire Management Organization. “The truth is that everywhere in Hawaii where humans go is at risk of ignition,” Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawai‘i Wildfire Management Organization said. While drier leeward communities are the most vulnerable to wildfire, windward towns are becoming increasingly susceptible as Hawaii experience more frequent periods of drought. University of Hawaii at Manoa wildfire expert Clay Trauernicht said the annual area burned in Hawaii has increased by 300% in the past decades, making the percentage area of land burned on par with fire-prone Western states such as California. The risk of wildfire in Hawaii is greater than 92% of states in the U.S., according to a U.S. Forest Service website called wildfirerisk.org. In recent years island wildfires have been helped by a relatively new fuel source: highly flammable non-native grasses like guinea grass and haole koa. These grassy savannas, University of Hawaii at Manoa wildfire expert Clay Trauernicht said, cover about a million acres across Hawaii and are mostly the legacy of land clearing for plantation agriculture and ranching in the late 1800s and early 1900s. Another ingredient in the wildfire mix is the climate. Steven Businger, UH Department of Atmospheric Sciences professor, said Hawaii are getting less rainfall, which is making Hawaii a drier place with conditions more suited for wildfire. On Aug. 8, wildfires on Maui and Hawaii were pushed by fierce winds driven by a phenomenon known as a downslope windstorm. a phenomenon known as a downslope windstorm occurred when a strong high pressure area north of the state interacted with an area of low pressure created by Hurricane Dora south of the state, causing winds to whip down the mountainsides with gusts of 60 mph or more. Steven Businger, UH Department of Atmospheric Sciences professor, said such downslope windstorms are notorious for occurring in the wintertime, but such downslope windstorms's frequency is likely to increase in the summer, when periods of drought and low humidity are more prevalent. On Aug. 8, conditions were ripe for wildfire, and historic Lahaina, with historic Lahaina, with its many wooden buildings and homes next to former sugar lands overrun by parched grass and brush's many wooden buildings and homes next to former sugar lands overrun by parched grass and brush, was an easy mark for the raging inferno. Wind-driven flames killed at least 115 people, destroyed more than 2,200 structures in the heart of historic Lahaina, with its many wooden buildings and homes next to former sugar lands overrun by parched grass and brush and caused an estimated $5.5 billion in damage. Following the disaster, communities across the state began to sit up and wonder if communities across the state are next. Pickett of the Hawai‘i Wildfire Management Organization said the services and expertise of Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawai‘i Wildfire Management Organization's small Big Island-based nonprofit are suddenly in high demand. “In the last few weeks the size of the Hawai‘i Wildfire Management Organization's program has doubled,” Elizabeth Pickett, co-director of the Hawai‘i Wildfire Management Organization said. “the disaster could have happened in our community,” said Michael Moran, association president. our community, Michael Moran, association president said, has many similarities to historic Lahaina, with its many wooden buildings and homes next to former sugar lands overrun by parched grass and brush, including the fact that our community is a dry, leeward community by the sea surrounded by former sugar cane fields. The our community fire on Aug. 8 burned right up to our community's northern edge and right up to the fencelines of 50-year-old wooden homes. And The Kihei fire on Aug. 8’s not the first time. But it’s the same thing, a short attention span. It was close, nothing happened, so no mitigation took place,” Michael Moran, association president said. Now, timeless structures and priceless artifacts are among the ruins of the disaster. the Hawaiian Mission Houses and the Hawaiian Mission Houses's partner the Lahaina Restoration Foundation still don’t have an exact inventory of the physical pieces destroyed in the disaster. It’s all part of a massive digitization project led by the Hawaiian Mission Houses and the Hawaiian Mission Houses’re also searching the Hawaiian Mission Houses's collection for any period furniture that could replace what was lost. While full recovery is years away, the hope is to use items relevant to Lahaina’s history including original photos, journals, drawings, and letter as a guide to help rebuild and also ensure Lahaina’s's history is shared worldwide and never forgotten. Hawaiian Electric is severely underinsured to pay victims of August’s deadly Maui wildfire, as well as repair Hawaiian Electric's own damaged infrastructure, raising questions over how Hawaiian Electric could cover billions of dollars in mounting damage claims if Hawaiian Electric is found liable for starting August’s deadly Maui wildfire. Sign up for the Climate Coach newsletter and get advice for life on our changing planet, in your inbox every Tuesday and Thursday. ArrowRight In a filing to state regulators on Monday, Hawaiian Electric said that Hawaiian Electric had $165 million in annual general liability insurance, which is what Hawaiian Electric would potentially use to help repay residents for residents's losses if it is determined that Hawaiian Electric's equipment started the deadly Lahaina fire. As of now, $165 million in annual general liability insurance, which is what it would potentially use to help repay residents for their losses would cover a small fraction of the potential $5 billion in damage claims, according to the research firm Capstone, which continue to grow as more residents and even Hawaiian Electric shareholders join the slew of lawsuits against Hawaiian Electric. Hawaiian Electric has said that Hawaiian Electric's equipment was responsible for starting a fire on the morning of Aug. 8 before Hawaiian Electric's lines were de-energized. Hawaiian Electric said that Maui County had declared a fire on the morning of Aug. 8 before its lines were de-energized “100% contained” and the fire chief later called a fire on the morning of Aug. 8 before its lines were de-energized “extinguished.” Hawaiian Electric has said there were two fires and that Hawaiian Electric is not at fault for the one that sparked that afternoon in the same area, which ultimately devastated Lahaina. While August’s deadly Maui wildfire is still under investigation, a spokesperson for the fire department and witnesses told The Post that August’s deadly Maui wildfire reignited amid high winds. Hawaiian Electric declined to comment Wednesday. Mike Kelly, a California wildfire attorney who leads an oversight committee related to Pacific Gas & Electric’s payouts to California's wildfire victims, said that Hawaiian Electric should have better analyzed Hawaiian Electric's risks when obtaining insurance, including the islands’ propensity for high winds and highly flammable grasses. Experts said it is common for utilities to have low amounts of liability insurance. It’s unclear whether Hawaiian Electric's general liability policy is what’s known as a “burning limits policy,” which means that attorneys’ fees from representing Hawaiian Electric would also come out of $165 million in annual general liability insurance, which is what it would potentially use to help repay residents for their losses if Hawaiian Electric were found liable, making the pool even smaller for victims. Many of these policies often are, experts said. Hawaiian Electric declined to answer a question about Hawaiian Electric's policy. a filing to state regulators on Monday shows that Hawaiian Electric also has property damage insurance that is limited in scope, with two policies totaling $500 million. However, within two policies totaling $500 million, there is a sublimit of just $5 million for fire damage to equipment such as power poles, lines, conductors and substations, many of which burned or were severely impacted by the August’s blaze. In comparison, Hawaiian Electric has a sublimit for at least $200 million in damage to Hawaiian Electric's own equipment from named wind storms. This stands out, experts who reviewed a filing to state regulators on Monday said, because it seriously underestimated the fire risk Hawaiian Electric faced despite highlighting the fire risk the utility faced in Hawaiian Electric's own 2023 Wildfire Mitigation Plan, which was also included in a filing to state regulators on Monday. its own 2023 Wildfire Mitigation Plan, which was also included in this week’s filing identified wildfire as a danger to West Maui, where Lahaina is located, and a top priority for Hawaiian Electric to address, but downplayed Hawaiian Electric's need to enact proactive mitigation measures, such as a power shut-off safety plan, and the flammability of the grasses around Hawaiian Electric's infrastructure. Hawaiian Electric created Hawaiian Electric's plan after studying California horrific 2018 Camp Fire, which killed at least 85 people, as well as Hawaiian Electric's own experiences with wildfire a year later. The primary objective of its own 2023 Wildfire Mitigation Plan, which was also included in this week’s filing was to “minimize the probability of Hawaiian Electric's facilities becoming the origin or contributing source of ignition for a wildfire,” Hawaiian Electric said. In 2019 and 2020, Hawaiian Electric used drones to those assessments Hawaiian Electric's facilities and the vegetation around Hawaiian Electric's equipment. Based on those assessments, Hawaiian Electric identified West Maui, where Lahaina is located, as priority area No. 1 for West Maui, where Lahaina is located. However, Hawaiian Electric made what experts said was a bizarre assessment, comparing Hawaiian Electric's risk to that of California as the reason for not having more frequent and robust inspections, vegetation management, and for not creating a public safety power shut-off program. “This is due to Hawaiian Electric having fewer facilities in high-risk wildfire areas and the islands’'s dry areas having very few tall trees, which is a major fuel source for wildfires, as compared to California,” its own 2023 Wildfire Mitigation Plan, which was also included in this week’s filing said. According to Hawaiian Electric, because Hawaiian Electric's wildfire risk was “significantly less than [in] California,” Hawaiian Electric needed to inspect Hawaiian Electric's transmission and substations only every three to five years, vs. annually. As for Hawaiian Electric's decision not to adopt a policy to preemptively shut off power in the face of red flag and high wind warnings, Hawaiian Electric said in Hawaiian Electric's 2023 wildfire mitigation plan that, “Based on news reports, Pacific Gas & Electric’s's practice to preemptively turn off circuits in certain areas if conditions were ripe for a wildfire was not well-received by certain customers affected.” Therefore, Hawaiian Electric felt that Hawaiian Electric did not need to adopt a policy to preemptively shut off power in the face of red flag and high wind warnings. “It’s clear and saddening, reading its own 2023 Wildfire Mitigation Plan, which was also included in this week’s filing, that Hawaiian Electric did not take the risk of consequence of ignition more clearly,” Wara said.
